<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreation">Recreation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Recreation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="504" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg/220px-Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg/330px-Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg/440px-Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Otto_Lilienthal" title="Otto Lilienthal">Otto Lilienthal</a> in flight</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wind figures prominently in several popular sports, including recreational <a href="/wiki/Hang_gliding" title="Hang gliding">hang gliding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_air_ballooning" title="Hot air ballooning">hot air ballooning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kite" title="Kite">kite flying</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snowkiting" title="Snowkiting">snowkiting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kite_landboarding" title="Kite landboarding">kite landboarding</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kite_surfing" title="Kite surfing">kite surfing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paragliding" title="Paragliding">paragliding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sailing" title="Sailing">sailing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Windsurfing" title="Windsurfing">windsurfing</a>. In gliding, wind gradients just above the surface affect the takeoff and landing phases of flight of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glider_aircraft" title="Glider aircraft">glider</a>. Wind gradient can have a noticeable effect on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_launch" title="Ground launch">ground launches</a>, also known as winch launches or wire launches. If the wind gradient is significant or sudden, or both, and the pilot maintains the same pitch attitude, the indicated airspeed will increase, possibly exceeding the maximum ground launch tow speed. The pilot must adjust the airspeed to deal with the effect of the gradient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> When landing, wind shear is also a hazard, particularly when the winds are strong. As the glider descends through the wind gradient on final approach to landing, airspeed decreases while sink rate increases, and there is insufficient time to accelerate prior to ground contact. The pilot must anticipate the wind gradient and use a higher approach speed to compensate for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piggott_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piggott-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_the_natural_world">Role in the natural world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Role in the natural world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In arid climates, the main source of erosion is wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erosion_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erosion-100">[100]</a></sup> The general wind circulation moves small particulates such as dust across wide oceans thousands of kilometers downwind of their point of origin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gobi_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gobi-101">[101]</a></sup> which is known as deflation. Westerly winds in the mid-latitudes of the planet drive the movement of ocean currents from west to east across the world's oceans. Wind has a very important role in aiding plants and other immobile organisms in dispersal of seeds, spores, pollen, etc. Although wind is not the primary form of seed dispersal in plants, it provides dispersal for a large percentage of the biomass of land plants.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Erosion">Erosion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Erosion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1544" data-file-width="2080" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg/220px-Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg/330px-Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg/440px-Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A rock formation in the <a href="/wiki/Altiplano" title="Altiplano">Altiplano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, sculpted by wind erosion</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aeolian_processes" title="Aeolian processes">Aeolian processes</a></div>
<p>Erosion can be the result of material movement by the wind. There are two main effects. First, wind causes small particles to be lifted and therefore moved to another region. This is called deflation. Second, these suspended particles may impact on solid objects causing erosion by abrasion (ecological succession). Wind erosion generally occurs in areas with little or no vegetation, often in areas where there is insufficient rainfall to support vegetation. An example is the formation of sand <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dunes" title="Dunes">dunes</a>, on a beach or in a desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Loess is a homogeneous, typically nonstratified, porous, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friable" title="Friable">friable</a>, slightly coherent, often calcareous, fine-grained, <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silty</a>, pale yellow or buff, windblown (Aeolian) <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> It generally occurs as a widespread blanket deposit that covers areas of hundreds of square kilometers and tens of meters thick. Loess often stands in either steep or vertical faces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Loess tends to develop into highly rich soils. Under appropriate climatic conditions, areas with loess are among the most agriculturally productive in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Loess deposits are geologically unstable by nature, and will erode very readily. Therefore, windbreaks (such as big trees and bushes) are often planted by farmers to reduce the wind erosion of loess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erosion_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erosion-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Desert_dust_migration">Desert dust migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Desert dust migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During mid-summer (July in the northern hemisphere), the westward-moving trade winds south of the northward-moving subtropical ridge expand northwestward from the Caribbean into southeastern North America. When dust from the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> moving around the southern periphery of the ridge within the belt of trade winds moves over land, rainfall is suppressed and the sky changes from a blue to a white appearance, which leads to an increase in red sunsets. Its presence negatively impacts <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_quality" title="Air quality">air quality</a> by adding to the count of airborne particulates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-106">[106]</a></sup> Over 50% of the African dust that reaches the United States affects Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Since 1970, dust outbreaks have worsened because of periods of drought in Africa. There is a large variability in the dust transport to the Caribbean and Florida from year to year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Dust events have been linked to a decline in the health of <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> across the Caribbean and Florida, primarily since the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Similar dust plumes originate in the <a href="/wiki/Gobi_Desert" title="Gobi Desert">Gobi Desert</a>, which combined with pollutants, spread large distances downwind, or eastward, into North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gobi_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gobi-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are local names for winds associated with sand and dust storms. The Calima carries dust on southeast winds into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canary_islands" title="Canary islands">Canary islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Harmattan" title="Harmattan">Harmattan</a> carries dust during the winter into the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Guinea" title="Gulf of Guinea">Gulf of Guinea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sirocco" title="Sirocco">Sirocco</a> brings dust from north Africa into southern Europe because of the movement of extratropical cyclones through the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Spring storm systems moving across the eastern Mediterranean Sea cause dust to carry across <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" title="Arabian peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a>, which are locally known as <a href="/wiki/Khamsin" title="Khamsin">Khamsin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Shamal_(wind)" title="Shamal (wind)">Shamal</a> is caused by cold fronts lifting dust into the atmosphere for days at a time across the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_plants">Effect on plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Effect on plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg/220px-Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg/330px-Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg/440px-Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tumbleweed blown against a fence</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG/220px-Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG/330px-Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG/440px-Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montane_ecology" title="Montane ecology">montane forest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Olympic_National_Park" title="Olympic National Park">Olympic National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windthrow" title="Windthrow">windthrow</a> opens the <a href="/wiki/Canopy_(biology)" title="Canopy (biology)">canopy</a> and increases light intensity on the <a href="/wiki/Understory" title="Understory">understory</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Seed_dispersal" title="Seed dispersal">Seed dispersal</a></div>
<p>Wind dispersal of seeds, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anemochory" title="Anemochory">anemochory</a>, is one of the more primitive means of dispersal. Wind dispersal can take on one of two primary forms: seeds can float on the breeze or alternatively, they can flutter to the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The classic examples of these dispersal mechanisms include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dandelion" title="Dandelion">dandelions</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Taraxacum" title="Taraxacum">Taraxacum</a></i> spp., <a href="/wiki/Asteraceae" title="Asteraceae">Asteraceae</a>), which have a feathery <a href="/wiki/Pappus_(flower_structure)" title="Pappus (flower structure)">pappus</a> attached to their seeds and can be dispersed long distances, and <a href="/wiki/Maple" title="Maple">maples</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acer_(genus)" title="Acer (genus)">Acer (genus)</a></i> spp., <a href="/wiki/Sapindaceae" title="Sapindaceae">Sapindaceae</a>), which have winged seeds and flutter to the ground. An important constraint on wind dispersal is the need for abundant seed production to maximize the likelihood of a seed landing in a site suitable for <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a>. There are also strong evolutionary constraints on this dispersal mechanism. For instance, species in the Asteraceae on islands tended to have reduced dispersal capabilities (i.e., larger seed mass and smaller pappus) relative to the same species on the mainland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Reliance upon wind dispersal is common among many <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">weedy</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruderal" title="Ruderal">ruderal</a> species. Unusual mechanisms of wind dispersal include <a href="/wiki/Tumbleweed" title="Tumbleweed">tumbleweeds</a>. A related process to anemochory is <a href="/wiki/Anemophily" title="Anemophily">anemophily</a>, which is the process where pollen is distributed by wind. Large families of plants are pollinated in this manner, which is favored when individuals of the dominant plant species are spaced closely together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wind also limits tree growth. On coasts and isolated mountains, the tree line is often much lower than in corresponding altitudes inland and in larger, more complex mountain systems, because strong winds reduce tree growth. High winds scour away thin <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a> through erosion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> as well as damage limbs and twigs. When high winds knock down or uproot trees, the process is known as <a href="/wiki/Windthrow" title="Windthrow">windthrow</a>. This is most likely on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Windward" title="Windward">windward</a> slopes of mountains, with severe cases generally occurring to <a href="/wiki/Tree_stand" title="Tree stand">tree stands</a> that are 75 years or older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Plant varieties near the coast, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sitka_spruce" title="Sitka spruce">Sitka spruce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coccoloba_uvifera" title="Coccoloba uvifera">sea grape</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> are <a href="/wiki/Pruning" title="Pruning">pruned</a> back by wind and salt spray near the coastline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wind can also cause plants damage through <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sand_abrasion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sand abrasion (page does not exist)">sand abrasion</a>. Strong winds will pick up loose sand and <a href="/wiki/Topsoil" title="Topsoil">topsoil</a> and hurl it through the air at speeds ranging from 25 miles per hour (40 km/h) to 40 miles per hour (64 km/h). Such windblown sand causes extensive damage to plant seedlings because it ruptures plant cells, making them vulnerable to evaporation and drought. Using a mechanical sandblaster in a laboratory setting, scientists affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Research_Service" title="Agricultural Research Service">Agricultural Research Service</a> studied the effects of windblown sand abrasion on cotton seedlings. The study showed that the seedlings responded to the damage created by the windblown sand abrasion by shifting energy from stem and root growth to the growth and repair of the damaged stems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> After a period of four weeks the growth of the seedling once again became uniform throughout the plant, as it was before the windblown sand abrasion occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_animals">Effect on animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Effect on animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a> are prone to <a href="/wiki/Wind_chill" title="Wind chill">wind chill</a> caused by a combination of wind and cold temperatures, when winds exceed 40 kilometers per hour (25 mph), rendering their hair and wool coverings ineffective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">penguins</a> use both a layer of <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">feathers</a> to help guard against coldness in both water and air, their <a href="/wiki/Flipper_(anatomy)" title="Flipper (anatomy)">flippers</a> and feet are less immune to the cold. In the coldest climates such as <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">emperor penguins</a> use <a href="/wiki/Kleptothermy" title="Kleptothermy">huddling</a> behavior to survive the wind and cold, continuously alternating the members on the outside of the assembled group, which reduces heat loss by 50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Flying <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, a subset of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthropods" title="Arthropods">arthropods</a>, are swept along by the prevailing winds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> while birds follow their own course taking advantage of wind conditions, in order to either fly or glide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> As such, fine line patterns within <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a> imagery, associated with converging winds, are dominated by insect returns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Bird migration, which tends to occur overnight within the lowest 7,000 feet (2,100 m) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>, contaminates wind profiles gathered by weather radar, particularly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WSR-88D" title="WSR-88D">WSR-88D</a>, by increasing the environmental wind returns by 15 knots (28 km/h) to 30 knots (56 km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pika" title="Pika">Pikas</a> use a wall of pebbles to store dry plants and grasses for the winter in order to protect the food from being blown away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cockroach" title="Cockroach">Cockroaches</a> use slight winds that precede the attacks of potential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predators</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Toad" title="Toad">toads</a>, to survive their encounters. Their <a href="/wiki/Cercus" title="Cercus">cerci</a> are very sensitive to the wind, and help them survive half of their attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elk" title="Elk">Elk</a> have a keen sense of smell that can detect potential upwind predators at a distance of 0.5 miles (800 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Increases in wind above 15 kilometers per hour (9.3 mph) signals <a href="/wiki/Glaucous_gull" title="Glaucous gull">glaucous gulls</a> to increase their foraging and aerial attacks on thick-billed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Murres" title="Murres">murres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sound_generation">Sound generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Sound generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wind causes the generation of sound. The movement of air causes movements of parts of natural objects, such as leaves or grass. These objects will produce sound if they touch each other. Even a soft wind will cause a low level of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_noise" title="Environmental noise">environmental noise</a>. If the wind is blowing harder, it may produce howling sounds of varying frequencies. This may be caused by the wind blowing over cavities, or by vortices created in the air downstream of an object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Especially on high buildings, many structural parts may be a cause of annoying noise at certain wind conditions. Examples of these parts are balconies, ventilation openings, roof openings or cables.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_damage">Related damage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Related damage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Severe_weather" title="Severe weather">Severe weather</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1832" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg/220px-Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg/330px-Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg/440px-Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Damage from <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Andrew" title="Hurricane Andrew">Hurricane Andrew</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>High winds are known to cause damage, depending upon the magnitude of their velocity and pressure differential. Wind pressures are positive on the windward side of a structure and negative on the leeward side. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Infrequent wind gusts can cause poorly designed <a href="/wiki/Suspension_bridge" title="Suspension bridge">suspension bridges</a> to sway. When wind gusts are at a similar frequency to the swaying of the bridge, the bridge can be destroyed more easily, such as what occurred with the <a href="/wiki/Tacoma_Narrows_Bridge_(1940)" title="Tacoma Narrows Bridge (1940)">Tacoma Narrows Bridge</a> in 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Wind speeds as low as 23 knots (43 km/h) can lead to power outages due to tree branches disrupting the flow of energy through power lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> While no species of tree is guaranteed to stand up to hurricane-force winds, those with shallow roots are more prone to uproot, and brittle trees such as <a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">eucalyptus</a>, sea <a href="/wiki/Hibiscus" title="Hibiscus">hibiscus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocado</a> are more prone to damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Hurricane-force winds cause substantial damage to mobile homes, and begin to structurally damage homes with foundations. Winds of this strength due to downsloped winds off terrain have been known to shatter windows and sandblast paint from cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boulder_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-boulder-53">[53]</a></sup> Once winds exceed 135 knots (250 km/h), homes completely collapse, and significant damage is done to larger buildings. Total destruction to man-made structures occurs when winds reach 175 knots (324 km/h). The <a href="/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_scale" title="Saffir–Simpson scale">Saffir–Simpson scale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_Fujita_scale" title="Enhanced Fujita scale">Enhanced Fujita scale</a> were designed to help estimate wind speed from the damage caused by high winds related to tropical cyclones and <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>, and vice versa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHC_SSHS_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHC_SSHS-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EF_SPC_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-EF_SPC-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Australia's <a href="/wiki/Barrow_Island_(Western_Australia)" title="Barrow Island (Western Australia)">Barrow Island</a> holds the record for the strongest wind gust, reaching 408 km/h (253 mph) during tropical cyclone Olivia on 10 April 1996, surpassing the previous record of 372 km/h (231 mph) set on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Washington_(New_Hampshire)" title="Mount Washington (New Hampshire)">Mount Washington (New Hampshire)</a> on the afternoon of 12 April 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The most powerful gusts of wind on Earth were created by nuclear detonations. The blast wave is similar to a strong wind gust over the ground. The largest nuclear explosion (50–58 megatons at an altitude of about 13,000 feet (4,000 m)) generated a 20 bar blast pressure at ground zero, which is similar to a wind gust of 3,100 miles per hour (5,000 km/h).</p>
<p>Wildfire intensity increases during daytime hours. For example, burn rates of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smoldering" title="Smoldering">smoldering</a> logs are up to five times greater during the day because of lower humidity, increased temperatures, and increased wind speeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Sunlight warms the ground during the day and causes air currents to travel uphill, and downhill during the night as the land cools. Wildfires are fanned by these winds and often follow the air currents over hills and through valleys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> United States wildfire operations revolve around a 24-hour <i>fire day</i> that begins at 10:00 a.m. because of the predictable increase in intensity resulting from the daytime warmth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_outer_space">In outer space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: In outer space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The solar wind is quite different from a terrestrial wind, in that its origin is the sun, and it is composed of charged particles that have escaped the sun's atmosphere. Similar to the solar wind, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_wind" title="Planetary wind">planetary wind</a> is composed of light gases that escape planetary atmospheres. Over long periods of time, the planetary wind can radically change the composition of planetary atmospheres.</p>
<p>The fastest wind ever recorded is coming from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accretion_disc" title="Accretion disc">accretion disc</a> of the <a href="/wiki/IGR_J17091-3624" title="IGR J17091-3624">IGR J17091-3624</a> black hole. Its speed is 20,000,000 miles per hour (32,000,000 km/h), which is 3% of the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_wind">Planetary wind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Planetary wind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_escape" title="Atmospheric escape">Atmospheric escape</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venuspioneeruv.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2158" data-file-width="2008" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/220px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/330px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/440px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venuspioneeruv.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Possible future for Earth due to the planetary wind: Venus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The hydrodynamic wind within the upper portion of a planet's atmosphere allows light chemical elements such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> to move up to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exobase" title="Exobase">exobase</a>, the lower limit of the <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exosphere</a>, where the gases can then reach <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a>, entering outer space without impacting other particles of gas. This type of gas loss from a planet into space is known as planetary wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Such a process over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geologic_time" title="Geologic time">geologic time</a> causes water-rich planets such as the Earth to evolve into planets like <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Additionally, planets with hotter lower atmospheres could accelerate the loss rate of hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dvorak_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dvorak-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_wind">Solar wind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Solar wind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">Solar wind</a></div>
<p>Rather than air, the solar wind is a <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">stream of charged particles</a>—a <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>—ejected from the <a href="/wiki/Stellar_atmosphere" title="Stellar atmosphere">upper atmosphere</a> of the sun at a rate of 400 kilometers per second (890,000 mph). It consists mostly of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrons" title="Electrons">electrons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> with energies of about 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_volt" title="Electron volt">keV</a>. The stream of particles varies in temperature and speed with the passage of time. These particles are able to escape the sun's <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, in part because of the high <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">corona</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> but also because of high <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> that particles gain through a process that is not well understood. The solar wind creates the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">Heliosphere</a>, a vast bubble in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> surrounding the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Planets require large magnetic fields in order to reduce the ionization of their upper atmosphere by the solar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dvorak_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dvorak-148">[148]</a></sup> Other phenomena caused by the solar wind include <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_storm" title="Geomagnetic storm">geomagnetic storms</a> that can knock out power grids on Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(phenomenon)" title="Aurora (phenomenon)">aurorae</a> such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">Northern Lights</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> and the plasma tails of <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a> that always point away from the sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="On_other_planets">On other planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: On other planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Strong 300 kilometers per hour (190 mph) winds at Venus's cloud tops circle the planet every four to five earth days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> When the poles of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> are exposed to sunlight after their winter, the frozen CO<sub>2</sub> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(physics)" title="Sublimation (physics)">sublimates</a>, creating significant winds that sweep off the poles as fast as 400 kilometers per hour (250 mph), which subsequently transports large amounts of dust and water vapor over its <a href="/wiki/Landscape" title="Landscape">landscape</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clouds_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-clouds-155">[155]</a></sup> Other Martian winds have resulted in <a href="/wiki/Cleaning_event" title="Cleaning event">cleaning events</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirit_rover#Dust_devils" title="Spirit rover">dust devils</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, wind speeds of 100 meters per second (220 mph) are common in zonal jet streams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Saturn's winds are among the Solar System's fastest. <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini–Huygens</a> data indicated peak easterly winds of 375 meters per second (840 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, northern hemisphere wind speeds reach as high as 240 meters per second (540 mph) near 50 degrees north latitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_.26_Fry_2005_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_.26_Fry_2005-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003.2C_2005_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003.2C_2005-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_Rages_et_al._2001_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_Rages_et_al._2001-162">[162]</a></sup> At the cloud tops of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, prevailing winds range in speed from 400 meters per second (890 mph) along the equator to 250 meters per second (560 mph) at the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-163">[163]</a></sup> At 70° S latitude on Neptune, a high-speed jet stream travels at a speed of 300 meters per second (670 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-164">[164]</a></sup> The fastest wind on any known planet is on <a href="/wiki/HD_80606_b" title="HD 80606 b">HD 80606 b</a> located 190 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_year" title="Light year">light years</a> away, where it blows at more than 11,000 mph or 5 km/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">JetStream (2008). <a class="external text" href="http://www.srh.noaa.gov/jetstream//synoptic/wind.htm" rel="nofollow">"Origin of Wind"</a>. <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> Southern Region Headquarters<span class="reference-accessdate">.
